font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>6</span>)</td></tr></tbody></table><b>Ices</b>:\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>3</span>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> (H<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>O)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>SH)</li></ul>\n</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Saturn</b> is the sixth <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> and the second-largest in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>, after <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant</a> with an average radius about nine times that of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radius_ref_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radius_ref-15">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Radius_ref_2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Radius_ref_2-16">[14]</a></sup> It has only one-eighth the average density of Earth, but with its larger volume Saturn is over 95 times more massive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mass_ref_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mass_ref-17">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mass_ref_2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mass_ref_2-18">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mass_ref_3_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mass_ref_3-19">[17]</a></sup> Saturn is named after the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">god of agriculture</a>; its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbol</a> (&#9796;) represents the god\'s <a href="/wiki/Sickle" title="Sickle">sickle</a>.\n</p><p>Saturn\'s interior is probably composed of a core of <a href="/wiki/Iron%E2%80%93nickel_alloy" title="Iron&#8211;nickel alloy">iron&#8211;nickel</a> and rock (<a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> compounds). This core is surrounded by a deep layer of <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">metallic hydrogen</a>, an intermediate layer of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">liquid helium</a>, and finally a gaseous outer layer. Saturn has a pale yellow hue due to <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> crystals in its upper atmosphere. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_current" title="Electrical current">Electrical current</a> within the metallic hydrogen layer is thought to give rise to Saturn\'s planetary <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>, which is weaker than Earth\'s, but has a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a> 580 times that of Earth due to Saturn\'s larger size. Saturn\'s magnetic field strength is around one-twentieth of Jupiter\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-mag-20">[18]</a></sup> The outer <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> is generally bland and lacking in contrast, although long-lived features can appear. <a href="/wiki/Wind_speed" title="Wind speed">Wind speeds</a> on Saturn can reach 1,800&#160;km/h (1,100&#160;mph; 500&#160;m/s), higher than on Jupiter, but not as high as those on <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_Ch._2004_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Ch._2004-21">[19]</a></sup> In January 2019, astronomers reported that a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daytime_(astronomy)" title="Daytime (astronomy)">day</a> on the planet Saturn has been determined to be <span class="nowrap">&#160;10<sup>h</sup>&#160;33<sup>m</sup>&#160;38<sup>s</sup> <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">+&#160;1<sup>m</sup>&#160;52<sup>s</sup></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">&#8722;&#160;1<sup>m</sup>&#160;19<sup>s</sup></sub></span>&#8201;</span>, based on studies of the planet\'s <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#C_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">C Ring</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20190118_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20190118-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-APJ-20190117_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-APJ-20190117-10">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The planet\'s most famous feature is its prominent <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">ring system</a> that is composed mostly of ice particles, with a smaller amount of rocky debris and <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">dust</a>. At least 62 <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">moons</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[20]</a></sup> are known to orbit Saturn, of which 53 are officially named. This does not include the hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Moonlet" title="Moonlet">moonlets</a> in the rings. <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, Saturn\'s largest moon, and the second-largest in the Solar System, is larger than the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, although less massive, and is the only moon in the Solar System to have a substantial atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Titan_ref_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Titan_ref-23">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872693506">.mw-parser-output .toclimit-2 .toclevel-1 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-3 .toclevel-2 ul,.mw-parser-output .toclimit-4 .toclevel-3 ul,.mw-parser-output id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2601" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Saturn%2C_Earth_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn,_Earth_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite image comparing the sizes of Saturn and Earth</div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn is a <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giant</a> because it is predominantly composed of hydrogen and helium. It lacks a definite surface, though it may have a solid core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-melosh2011_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-melosh2011-24">[22]</a></sup> Saturn\'s rotation causes it to have the shape of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oblate_spheroid" title="Oblate spheroid">oblate spheroid</a>; that is, it is <a href="/wiki/Flattening" title="Flattening">flattened</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">poles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_bulge" title="Equatorial bulge">bulges</a> at its <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>. Its equatorial and polar radii differ by almost 10%: 60,268&#160;km versus 54,364&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> Jupiter, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, and Neptune, the other giant planets in the Solar System, are also oblate but to a lesser extent. The combination of the bulge and rotation rate means that the effective surface gravity along the equator, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000896000000000000&#9824;</span>8.96&#160;m/s<sup>2</sup></span>, is 74% that at the poles and is lower than the surface gravity of Earth. However, the equatorial <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">escape velocity</a> of nearly <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7004360000000000000&#9824;</span>36&#160;km/s</span> is much higher than that for Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregersen2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregersen2010-25">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Saturn is the only planet of the Solar System that is less dense than water&#8212;about 30% less.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-preserve_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-preserve-26">[24]</a></sup> Although Saturn\'s <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> is considerably denser than water, the average <a href="/wiki/Relative_density" title="Relative density">specific density</a> of the planet is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002690000000000000&#9824;</span>0.69&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span> due to the atmosphere. Jupiter has 318 times <a href="/wiki/Earth_mass" title="Earth mass">Earth\'s mass</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jupiter_fact_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jupiter_fact-27">[25]</a></sup> and Saturn is 95 times Earth\'s mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> Together, Jupiter and Saturn hold 92% of the total planetary mass in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr152_1_423_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssr152_1_423-28">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Saturn_diagram.svg/220px-Saturn_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Saturn_diagram.svg/330px-Saturn_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Saturn_diagram.svg/440px-Saturn_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of Saturn, to scale</div></div></div>\n<p>Despite consisting mostly of hydrogen and helium, most of Saturn\'s mass is not in the <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phase</a>, because hydrogen becomes a <a href="/wiki/Ideal_solution" title="Ideal solution">non-ideal liquid</a> when the density is above <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001100000000000000&#9824;</span>0.01&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup></span>, which is reached at a radius containing 99.9% of Saturn\'s mass. The temperature, pressure, and density inside Saturn all rise steadily toward the core, which causes hydrogen to be a metal in the deeper layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssr152_1_423_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-ssr152_1_423-28">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Standard planetary models suggest that the interior of Saturn is similar to that of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, having a small rocky core surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a> with trace amounts of various <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot_et_al2009_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-guillot_et_al2009-29">[27]</a></sup> This core is similar in composition to Earth, but more dense. Examination of Saturn\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_moment" title="Gravitational moment">gravitational moment</a>, in combination with physical models of the interior, has allowed constraints to be placed on the mass of Saturn\'s core. In 2004, scientists estimated that the core must be 9&#8211;22 times the mass of Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science305_5689_1414_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-science305_5689_1414-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj609_2_1170_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj609_2_1170-31">[29]</a></sup> which corresponds to a diameter of about 25,000&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup> This is surrounded by a thicker liquid <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">metallic hydrogen</a> layer, followed by a liquid layer of helium-saturated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen">molecular hydrogen</a> that gradually transitions to a gas with increasing altitude. The outermost layer spans 1,000&#160;km and consists of gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faure_mensing2007_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-faure_mensing2007-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Saturn has a hot interior, reaching 11,700&#160;&#176;C at its core, and it radiates 2.5 times more energy into space than it receives from the Sun. Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> is generated by the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin%E2%80%93Helmholtz_mechanism" title="Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism">Kelvin&#8211;Helmholtz mechanism</a> of slow <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_compression" title="Gravitational compression">gravitational compression</a>, but such a process alone may not be sufficient to explain heat production for Saturn, because it is less massive. An alternative or additional mechanism may be generation of heat through the "raining out" of droplets of helium deep in Saturn\'s interior. As the droplets descend through the lower-density hydrogen, the process releases heat by <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> and leaves Saturn\'s outer layers depleted of helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_pater_lissauer2010_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_pater_lissauer2010-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_saturn_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_saturn-37">[35]</a></sup> These descending droplets may have accumulated into a helium shell surrounding the core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot_et_al2009_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-guillot_et_al2009-29">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">Rainfalls</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diamond" title="Diamond">diamonds</a> have been suggested to occur within Saturn, as well as in Jupiter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SC-20131009_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-SC-20131009-38">[36]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a> <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170825_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170825-39">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg/220px-PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg/330px-PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg/440px-PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18354-Saturn-MethaneBands-20150906.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a> bands circle Saturn. The moon Dione hangs below the rings to the right.</div></div></div>\n<p>The outer atmosphere of Saturn contains 96.3% molecular hydrogen and 3.25% helium by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> The proportion of helium is significantly deficient compared to the abundance of this element in the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guillot_et_al2009_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-guillot_et_al2009-29">[27]</a></sup> The quantity of elements heavier than helium (<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>) is not known precisely, but the proportions are assumed to match the primordial abundances from the formation of the Solar System. The total mass of these heavier elements is estimated to be 19&#8211;31 times the mass of the Earth, with a significant fraction located in Saturn\'s core region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science286_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-science286-41">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Trace amounts of ammonia, <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphine" title="Phosphine">phosphine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> have been detected in Saturn\'s atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baas15_831_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-baas15_831-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2009_24029_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2009_24029-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfsaa2008_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pfsaa2008-44">[42]</a></sup> The upper clouds are composed of ammonia crystals, while the lower level clouds appear to consist of either <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>SH) or water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martinez20050905_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-martinez20050905-45">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> radiation from the Sun causes methane <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> in the upper atmosphere, leading to a series of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> chemical reactions with the resulting products being carried downward by eddies and diffusion. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_cycle" title="Photochemical cycle">photochemical cycle</a> is modulated by Saturn\'s annual seasonal cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfsaa2008_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-pfsaa2008-44">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cloud_layers">Cloud layers</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Storm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1015" data-file-width="1015" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Saturn_Storm.jpg/220px-Saturn_Storm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Saturn_Storm.jpg/330px-Saturn_Storm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Saturn_Storm.jpg/440px-Saturn_Storm.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Storm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A global storm girdles the planet in 2011. The head of the storm (bright area) passes the tail circling around the left limb.</div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn\'s atmosphere exhibits a banded pattern similar to Jupiter\'s, but Saturn\'s bands are much fainter and are much wider near the equator. The nomenclature used to describe these bands is the same as on Jupiter. Saturn\'s finer cloud patterns were not observed until the flybys of the <i>Voyager</i> spacecraft during the 1980s. Since then, Earth-based <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopy</a> has improved to the point where regular observations can be made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emp105_2_143_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-emp105_2_143-46">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The composition of the clouds varies with depth and increasing pressure. In the upper cloud layers, with the temperature in the range 100&#8211;160&#160;K and pressures extending between 0.5&#8211;2 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a>, the clouds consist of ammonia ice. Water <a href="/wiki/Ice_cloud" title="Ice cloud">ice clouds</a> begin at a level where the pressure is about 2.5 bar and extend down to 9.5 bar, where temperatures range from 185&#8211;270&#160;K. Intermixed in this layer is a band of ammonium hydrosulfide ice, lying in the pressure range 3&#8211;6 bar with temperatures of 190&#8211;235&#160;K. Finally, the lower layers, where pressures are between 10&#8211;20 bar and temperatures are 270&#8211;330&#160;K, contains a region of water droplets with ammonia in aqueous solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dougherty_esposito2009_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-dougherty_esposito2009-47">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Saturn\'s usually bland atmosphere occasionally exhibits long-lived ovals and other features common on Jupiter. In 1990, the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> imaged an enormous white cloud near Saturn\'s equator that was not present during the <i>Voyager</i> encounters, and in 1994 another smaller storm was observed. The 1990 storm was an example of a <a href="/wiki/Great_White_Spot" title="Great White Spot">Great White Spot</a>, a unique but short-lived phenomenon that occurs once every Saturnian year, roughly every 30 Earth years, around the time of the northern hemisphere\'s <a href="/wiki/Summer_solstice" title="Summer solstice">summer solstice</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-icarus176_1_155_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-icarus176_1_155-48">[46]</a></sup> Previous Great White Spots were observed in 1876, 1903, 1933 and 1960, with the 1933 storm being the most famous. If the periodicity is maintained, another storm will occur in about 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The winds on Saturn are the second fastest among the Solar System\'s planets, after Neptune\'s. <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager</a> data indicate peak easterly winds of 500&#160;m/s (1,800&#160;km/h).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_Summary_1_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager_Summary_1-50">[48]</a></sup> In images from the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i> spacecraft during 2007, Saturn\'s northern hemisphere displayed a bright blue hue, similar to Uranus. The color was most likely caused by <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thermography" title="Thermography">Thermography</a> has shown that Saturn\'s south pole has a warm <a href="/wiki/Polar_vortex" title="Polar vortex">polar vortex</a>, the only known example of such a phenomenon in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCP_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCP-52">[50]</a></sup> Whereas temperatures on Saturn are normally &#8722;185&#160;&#176;C, temperatures on the vortex often reach as high as &#8722;122&#160;&#176;C, suspected to be the warmest spot on Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCP_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-MCP-52">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="North_pole_hexagonal_cloud_pattern">North pole hexagonal cloud pattern</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_hexagon" title="Saturn\'s hexagon">Saturn\'s hexagon</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:197px;max-width:197px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:195px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="384" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif/195px-Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif/293px-Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Rotatingsaturnhexagon.gif 2x" width="195"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Saturn\'s north pole (<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a> animation)</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:191px;max-width:191px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:195px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg/189px-Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg/284px-Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg/378px-Looking_saturn_in_the_eye.jpg 2x" width="189"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Saturn\'s south pole</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>A persisting <a href="/wiki/Hexagon" title="Hexagon">hexagonal</a> wave pattern around the north polar vortex in the atmosphere at about 78&#176;N was first noted in the Voyager images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20140806_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20140806-55">[53]</a></sup> The sides of the hexagon are each about 13,800&#160;km (8,600&#160;mi) long, which is longer than the diameter of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hexagon_ref_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hexagon_ref-56">[54]</a></sup> The entire structure rotates with a period of <span class="nowrap">10h 39m 24s</span> (the same period as that of the planet\'s radio emissions) which is assumed to be equal to the period of rotation of Saturn\'s interior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science247_4947_1206_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-science247_4947_1206-57">[55]</a></sup> The hexagonal feature does not shift in longitude like the other clouds in the visible atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pss57_14_1671_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-pss57_14_1671-58">[56]</a></sup> The pattern\'s origin is a matter of much speculation. Most scientists think  it is a <a href="/wiki/Standing_wave" title="Standing wave">standing wave</a> pattern in the atmosphere. Polygonal shapes have been replicated in the laboratory through differential rotation of fluids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-labmodel_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-labmodel-60">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="South_pole_vortex">South pole vortex</span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">HST</a> imaging of the south polar region indicates the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Jet_stream" title="Jet stream">jet stream</a>, but no strong polar vortex nor any hexagonal standing wave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> reported in November 2006 that <i>Cassini</i> had observed a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hurricane" title="Hurricane">hurricane</a>-like" storm locked to the south pole that had a clearly defined <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyewall" title="Eyewall">eyewall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> Eyewall clouds had not previously been seen on any planet other than Earth. For example, images from the <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo</a></i> spacecraft did not show an eyewall in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> of Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The south pole storm may have been present for billions of years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spole_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-spole-65">[63]</a></sup> This vortex is comparable to the size of Earth, and it has winds of 550&#160;km/h.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spole_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-spole-65">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_features">Other features</span></h4>\n<p><i>Cassini</i> observed a series of cloud features nicknamed "String of Pearls" found in northern latitudes. These features are cloud clearings that reside in deeper cloud layers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Saturn" title="Magnetosphere of Saturn">Magnetosphere of Saturn</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:151px;max-width:151px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:148px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_double_aurorae_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="149"
