2x" width="149"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Polar aurorae on Saturn</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:237px;max-width:237px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:148px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2222" data-file-width="3519" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg/235px-Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg/353px-Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg/470px-Hubble_sees_a_flickering_light_display_on_Saturn.jpg 2x" width="235"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Auroral lights at Saturn\'s north pole<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="72.409977324263" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Saturn_sound2.ogg" data-startoffset="1.1508390022676" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:220px"><source data-bandwidth="34145" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (34 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/Saturn_sound2.ogg" type=\'audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"\'/><source data-bandwidth="69176" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-title="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/90/Saturn_sound2.ogg/Saturn_sound2.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg"/></audio></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_sound2.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Radio emissions detected by <i>Cassini</i></div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn has an intrinsic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic" title="Magnetic">magnetic</a> field that has a simple, symmetric shape &#8211; a magnetic <a href="/wiki/Dipole" title="Dipole">dipole</a>. Its strength at the equator &#8211; 0.2&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">gauss</a> (20&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microtesla" title="Microtesla">&#181;T</a>) &#8211; is approximately one twentieth of that of the field around Jupiter and slightly weaker than Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-mag-20">[18]</a></sup> As a result, Saturn\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> is much smaller than Jupiter\'s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_2_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-mag_2-68">[66]</a></sup> When <i>Voyager 2</i> entered the magnetosphere, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> pressure was high and the magnetosphere extended only 19 Saturn radii, or 1.1 million km (712,000&#160;mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup> although it enlarged within several hours, and remained so for about three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atkinson2010_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-atkinson2010-70">[68]</a></sup> Most probably, the magnetic field is generated similarly to that of Jupiter &#8211; by currents in the liquid metallic-hydrogen layer called a metallic-hydrogen dynamo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_2_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-mag_2-68">[66]</a></sup> This magnetosphere is efficient at deflecting the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> particles from the Sun. The moon Titan orbits within the outer part of Saturn\'s magnetosphere and contributes plasma from the <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionized</a> particles in Titan\'s outer atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mag_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-mag-20">[18]</a></sup> Saturn\'s magnetosphere, like <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth\'s magnetic field">Earth\'s</a>, produces <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurorae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1020" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg/220px-PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg/330px-PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg/440px-PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA20517-Saturn%26Rings-CassiniSpacecraftScene-20161028.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saturn and rings as viewed by the <i>Cassini</i> spacecraft (28 October 2016)</div></div></div>\n<p>The average distance between Saturn and the Sun is over 1.4 billion kilometers (9&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a>). With an average orbital speed of 9.68&#160;km/s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> it takes Saturn 10,759 Earth days (or about &#8203;<span class="frac nowrap">29<span class="visualhide">&#160;</span><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span>&#160;years)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2009_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2009-72">[70]</a></sup> to finish one revolution around the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> As a consequence, it forms a near 5:2 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mean-motion_resonance" title="Mean-motion resonance">mean-motion resonance</a> with Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> The elliptical orbit of Saturn is inclined 2.48&#176; relative to the orbital plane of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion and aphelion</a> distances are, respectively, 9.195 and 9.957&#160;AU, on average.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-fact-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup> The visible features on Saturn rotate at different rates depending on latitude and multiple rotation periods have been assigned to various regions (as in Jupiter\'s case).\n</p><p>Astronomers use three different systems for specifying the rotation rate of Saturn. <i>System&#160;I</i> has a period of 10&#160;hr 14&#160;min 00&#160;sec (844.3&#176;/d) and encompasses the Equatorial Zone, the South Equatorial Belt and the North Equatorial Belt. The polar regions are considered to have rotation rates similar to <i>System&#160;I</i>. All other Saturnian latitudes, excluding the north and south polar regions, are indicated as <i>System&#160;II</i> and have been assigned a rotation period of 10&#160;hr 38&#160;min 25.4&#160;sec (810.76&#176;/d). <i>System&#160;III</i> refers to Saturn\'s internal rotation rate. Based on <a href="/wiki/Radio_astronomy" title="Radio astronomy">radio emissions</a> from the planet detected by Voyager 1 and Voyager 2,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup> System&#160;III has a rotation period of 10&#160;hr 39&#160;min 22.4&#160;sec (810.8&#176;/d). System&#160;III has largely superseded System&#160;II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-benton2006_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-benton2006-76">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A precise value for the rotation period of the interior remains elusive. While approaching Saturn in 2004, <i>Cassini</i> found that the radio rotation period of Saturn had increased appreciably, to approximately 10&#160;hr 45&#160;min 45&#160;sec (&#177;&#160;36&#160;sec).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_15298_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_15298-78">[76]</a></sup> The latest estimate of Saturn\'s rotation (as an indicated rotation rate for Saturn as a whole) based on a compilation of various measurements from the <i>Cassini</i>, <i>Voyager</i> and Pioneer probes was reported in September 2007 is 10&#160;hr 32&#160;min 35&#160;sec.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson2007_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2007-79">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 2007, it was found that the variation of radio emissions from the planet did not match Saturn\'s rotation rate. This variance may be caused by geyser activity on Saturn\'s moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>. The water vapor emitted into Saturn\'s orbit by this activity becomes charged and creates a drag upon Saturn\'s magnetic field, slowing its rotation slightly relative to the rotation of the planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science316_5823_442_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-science316_5823_442-81">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science316_5823_380_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-science316_5823_380-82">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An apparent oddity for Saturn is that it does not have any known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid">trojan asteroids</a>. These are minor planets that orbit the Sun at the stable <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a>, designated L<sub>4</sub> and L<sub>5</sub>, located at 60&#176; angles to the planet along its orbit. Trojan asteroids have been discovered for Mars, Jupiter, Uranus, and Neptune. Orbital <a href="/wiki/Resonance" title="Resonance">resonance</a> mechanisms, including <a href="/wiki/Secular_resonance" title="Secular resonance">secular resonance</a>, are believed to be the cause of the missing Saturnian trojans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural_satellites">Natural satellites</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">Moons of Saturn</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2015" data-file-width="2351" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg/220px-Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg/330px-Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg/440px-Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_System_Montage_-_GPN-2000-000439.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A montage of Saturn and its principal <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> (<a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a> not shown). This famous image was created from photographs taken in November 1980 by the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> spacecraft.</div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn has 62 known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, 53 of which have formal names.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JPLmoons_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-JPLmoons-84">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup> In addition, there is evidence of dozens to hundreds of moonlets with diameters of 40&#8211;500 meters in Saturn\'s rings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> which are not considered to be true moons. <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, the largest moon, comprises more than 90% of the mass in orbit around Saturn, including the rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brunier2005_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-brunier2005-87">[85]</a></sup> Saturn\'s second-largest moon, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, may have a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Rhea" title="Rings of Rhea">ring system of its own</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2008_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones2008-88">[86]</a></sup> along with a tenuous <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atkinson20101126_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-atkinson20101126-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ryan20101126_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-ryan20101126-91">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="496" data-file-width="496" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/220px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/330px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg/440px-PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18078-PossibleBeginning-NewMoonOfPlanetSaturn-20130415.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Possible beginning of a new moon (white dot) of Saturn (image taken by <i>Cassini</i> on 15 April 2013)</div></div></div>\n<p>Many of the other moons are small: 34 are less than 10&#160;km in diameter and another 14 between 10 and 50&#160;km in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_moons_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_moons-92">[90]</a></sup> Traditionally, most of Saturn\'s moons have been named after <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">Titans</a> of Greek mythology. Titan is the only satellite in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> with a major <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">atmosphere</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> in which a complex organic chemistry occurs. It is the only satellite with <a href="/wiki/Lakes_of_Titan" title="Lakes of Titan">hydrocarbon lakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 6 June 2013, scientists at the <a href="/wiki/Instituto_de_Astrof%C3%ADsica_de_Andaluc%C3%ADa" title="Instituto de Astrof&#237;sica de Andaluc&#237;a">IAA-CSIC</a> reported the detection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_atmosphere" title="Upper atmosphere">upper atmosphere</a> of Titan, a <a href="/wiki/PAH_world_hypothesis" title="PAH world hypothesis">possible precursor for life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAA-20130606_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-IAA-20130606-97">[95]</a></sup> On 23 June 2014, NASA claimed to have strong evidence that <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> in the atmosphere of Titan came from materials in the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comets" title="Comets">comets</a>, and not from the materials that formed Saturn in earlier times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-201450623_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-201450623-98">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Saturn\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, which seems similar in chemical makeup to comets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS-20080326_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-NS-20080326-99">[97]</a></sup> has often been regarded as a potential <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">habitat</a> for <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbial life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madrigal20090624_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-madrigal20090624-101">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotts20050928_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-spotts20050928-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> Evidence of this possibility includes the satellite\'s salt-rich particles having an "ocean-like" composition that indicates most of Enceladus\'s expelled <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">ice</a> comes from the evaporation of liquid salt water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> A 2015 flyby by <i>Cassini</i> through a plume on Enceladus found most of the ingredients to sustain life forms that live by methanogenesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa20170413_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-nasa20170413-107">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April 2014, NASA scientists reported the possible beginning of a new moon within the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#A_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">A Ring</a>, which was imaged by <i>Cassini</i> on 15 April 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140414a_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140414a-108">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_rings">Planetary rings</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">Rings of Saturn</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;max-width:442px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:217px;max-width:217px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:152px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_(2007-01-19).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2908" data-file-width="4088" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg/215px-Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg/323px-Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg/430px-Saturn_from_Cassini_Orbiter_%282007-01-19%29.jpg 2x" width="215"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">rings of Saturn</a> (imaged here by <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i> in 2007) are the most massive and conspicuous in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-34">[32]</a></sup></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:221px;max-width:221px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:152px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="691" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg/219px-Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg/329px-Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg/438px-Saturn%27s_A_Ring_From_the_Inside_Out.jpg 2x" width="219"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">False-color <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV" title="UV">UV</a> image of Saturn\'s outer <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#B_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">B</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#A_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">A</a> rings; dirtier ringlets in the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#Cassini_Division" title="Rings of Saturn">Cassini Division</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#Encke_Gap" title="Rings of Saturn">Encke Gap</a> show up red.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn is probably best known for the system of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a> that makes it visually unique.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-34">[32]</a></sup> The rings extend from 6,630 to 120,700 kilometers (4,120 to 75,000&#160;mi) outward from Saturn\'s equator and average approximately 20 meters (66&#160;ft) in thickness. They are composed predominantly of water ice with trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholin</a> impurities, and a peppered coating of approximately 7% amorphous <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup> The particles that make up the rings range in size from specks of dust up to 10&#160;m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> While the other <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a> also have ring systems, Saturn\'s is the largest and most visible.\n</p><p>There are two main hypotheses regarding the origin of the rings. One hypothesis is that the rings are remnants of a destroyed moon of Saturn. The second hypothesis is that the rings are left over from the original <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebular</a> material from which Saturn formed. Some ice in the E ring comes from the moon Enceladus\'s geysers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spahn_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spahn-111">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[112]</a></sup> The water abundance of the rings vary radially, with the outermost ring A being the most pure in ice water. This abundance variance may be explained by meteor bombardment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Beyond the main rings at a distance of 12 million km from the planet is the sparse Phoebe ring, which is tilted at an angle of 27&#176; to the other rings and, like <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a>, orbits in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_motion" title="Retrograde motion">retrograde</a> fashion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some of the moons of Saturn, including <a href="/wiki/Pandora_(moon)" title="Pandora (moon)">Pandora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(moon)" title="Prometheus (moon)">Prometheus</a>, act as shepherd moons to confine the rings and prevent them from spreading out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-russell2004_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-russell2004-117">[115]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pan_(moon)" title="Pan (moon)">Pan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(moon)" title="Atlas (moon)">Atlas</a> cause weak, linear density waves in Saturn\'s rings that have yielded more reliable calculations of their masses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_observation_and_exploration">History of observation and exploration</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1180" decoding="async" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/180px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/270px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/360px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> first observed the rings of Saturn in 1610</div></div></div>\n<p>The observation and exploration of Saturn can be divided into three main phases. The first era was ancient observations (such as with the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>), before the invention of the modern <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a>. Starting in the 17th century, progressively more advanced telescopic observations from Earth have been made. The third phase is visitation by <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probes</a>, by either orbiting or <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a>. In the 21st century, observations continue from Earth (including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth_orbit" title="Earth orbit">Earth-orbiting</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_telescope" title="Space telescope">observatories</a> like the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a>) and, until <a href="/wiki/Cassini_retirement" title="Cassini retirement">its 2017 retirement</a>, from the <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i> orbiter around Saturn.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_observations">Ancient observations</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn (mythology)</a></div>\n<p>Saturn has been known since prehistoric times<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_2_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn_2-119">[117]</a></sup> and in early recorded history it was a major character in various mythologies. <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomy" title="Babylonian astronomy">Babylonian astronomers</a> systematically observed and recorded the movements of Saturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ptrsl276_1257_43_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ptrsl276_1257_43-120">[118]</a></sup> In ancient Greek, the planet was known as <b>Phainon</b>, and in Roman times it was known as the "star of <a href="/wiki/Saturn_(mythology)" title="Saturn (mythology)">Saturn</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[119]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">ancient Roman mythology</a>, the planet Phainon was sacred to this agricultural god, from which the planet takes its modern name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies-122">[120]</a></sup> The Romans considered the god Saturnus the equivalent of the <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek god</a> <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a>; in modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, the planet retains the name <i>Cronus</i>&#8212;&#922;&#961;&#972;&#957;&#959;&#962;: <i>Kronos</i>.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Greek scientist <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> based his calculations of Saturn\'s orbit on observations he made while it was in <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps04_1893_862_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-ps04_1893_862-124">[122]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Jyotisha" title="Jyotisha">Hindu astrology</a>, there are nine astrological objects, known as <a href="/wiki/Navagraha" title="Navagraha">Navagrahas</a>. Saturn is known as "<a href="/wiki/Shani" title="Shani">Shani</a>" and judges everyone based on the good and bad deeds performed in life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Saturn_in_ancient_mythologies-122">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps04_1893_862_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-ps04_1893_862-124">[122]</a></sup> Ancient <a href="/wiki/Chinese_astrology" title="Chinese astrology">Chinese</a> and Japanese culture designated the planet Saturn as the "earth star" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#22303;&#26143;</span>). This was based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_elements_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Five elements (Chinese philosophy)">Five Elements</a> which were traditionally used to classify natural elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[125]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In ancient <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, Saturn is called \'Shabbathai\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[126]</a></sup> Its angel is <a href="/wiki/Cassiel" title="Cassiel">Cassiel</a>. Its intelligence or beneficial spirit is <a href="/wiki/Agiel" title="Agiel">\'Ag&#523;&#519;l</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">&#1506;&#1458;&#1494;&#1464;&#1488;&#1494;&#1461;&#1500;</span>&#8206;, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Hebrew" title="Romanization of Hebrew">translit.</a>&#160;</small><i lang="he-Latn" title="Hebrew-language transliteration">&#703;Agyal</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMagus_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheMagus-129">[127]</a></sup> and its darker spirit (<a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">demon</a>) is <a href="/wiki/Zazel_(spirit)" title="Zazel (spirit)">Z&#515;z&#519;l</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="he">&#1494;&#1488;&#1494;&#1500;</span>&#8206;, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Hebrew" title="Romanization of Hebrew">translit.</a>&#160;</small><i lang="he-Latn" title="Hebrew-language transliteration">Zazl</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMagus_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-TheMagus-129">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CrystalLinks_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-CrystalLinks-130">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TC-20170308_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-TC-20170308-131">[129]</a></sup> Zazel has been described as <i>a <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">great angel</a>, invoked in <a href="/wiki/Solomon#Angels_and_magic" title="Solomon">Solomonic magic</a>, who is "effective in <a href="/wiki/Evocation" title="Evocation">love conjurations</a>"</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FE-2014_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FE-2014-132">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HP-1998_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-HP-1998-133">[131]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>, the name of Zazel is \'Zuhal\', derived from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">&#1586;&#1581;&#1604;</span>&#8206;, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">translit.</a>&#160;</small><i lang="ar-Latn" title="Arabic-language transliteration">Zuhal</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CrystalLinks_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-CrystalLinks-130">[128]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="European_observations_.2817th.E2.80.9319th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="European_observations_(17th&#8211;19th_centuries)">European observations (17th&#8211;19th centuries)</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="594" data-file-width="886" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg/220px-Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg/330px-Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg/440px-Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_Robert_Hooke_1666.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">Robert Hooke</a> noted the shadows (a and b) cast by both the globe and the rings on each other in this drawing of Saturn in 1666.</div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn\'s rings require at least a 15-mm-diameter <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-binoculars_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-binoculars-134">[132]</a></sup> to resolve and thus were not known to exist until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a> first saw them in 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chan2000_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-chan2000-135">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_15390_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_15390-136">[134]</a></sup> He thought of them as two moons on Saturn\'s sides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_15418_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_15418-137">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cain2008_46237_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-cain2008_46237-138">[136]</a></sup> It was not until <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> used greater telescopic magnification that this notion was refuted. Huygens discovered Saturn\'s moon Titan; <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Domenico Cassini</a> later discovered four other moons: <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">Iapetus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(moon)" title="Rhea (moon)">Rhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tethys_(moon)" title="Tethys (moon)">Tethys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">Dione</a>. In 1675, Cassini discovered the gap now known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_Division" title="Cassini Division">Cassini Division</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-micek2007_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-micek2007-139">[137]</a></sup>\n</p><p>No further discoveries of significance were made until 1789 when <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a> discovered two further moons, <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>. The irregularly shaped satellite <a href="/wiki/Hyperion_(moon)" title="Hyperion (moon)">Hyperion</a>, which has a <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Titan, was discovered in 1848 by a British team.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa54_122_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-pa54_122-140">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1899 <a href="/wiki/William_Henry_Pickering" title="William Henry Pickering">William Henry Pickering</a> discovered Phoebe, a highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">irregular satellite</a> that does not rotate synchronously with Saturn as the larger moons do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pa54_122_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-pa54_122-140">[138]</a></sup> Phoebe was the first such satellite found and it takes more than a year to orbit Saturn in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a>. During the early 20th century, research on Titan led to the confirmation in 1944 that it had a thick atmosphere &#8211; a feature unique among the Solar System\'s moons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj100_378_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj100_378-141">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_NASA_and_ESA_probes">Modern NASA and ESA probes</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Saturn" title="Exploration of Saturn">Exploration of Saturn</a></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pioneer_11_flyby"><i>Pioneer 11</i> flyby</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P11saturnb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="373" data-file-width="646" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/P11saturnb.jpg/220px-P11saturnb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/P11saturnb.jpg/330px-P11saturnb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/P11saturnb.jpg/440px-P11saturnb.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P11saturnb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Pioneer 11</i> image of Saturn</div></div></div>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">Pioneer 11</a></i> made the first flyby of Saturn in September 1979, when it passed within 20,000&#160;km of the planet\'s cloud tops. Images were taken of the planet and a few of its moons, although their resolution was too low to discern surface detail. The spacecraft also studied Saturn\'s rings, revealing the thin F-ring and the fact that dark gaps in the rings are bright when viewed at high <a href="/wiki/Phase_angle_(astronomy)" title="Phase angle (astronomy)">phase angle</a> (towards the Sun), meaning that they contain fine light-scattering material. In addition, Pioneer 11 measured the temperature of Titan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[140]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Voyager_flybys">Voyager flybys</span></h4>\n<p>In November 1980, the <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> probe visited the Saturn system. It sent back the first high-resolution images of the planet, its rings and satellites. Surface features of various moons were seen for the first time. <i>Voyager 1</i> performed a close flyby of Titan, increasing knowledge of the atmosphere of the moon. It proved that Titan\'s atmosphere is impenetrable in visible wavelengths; therefore no surface details were seen. The flyby changed the spacecraft\'s trajectory out from the plane of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-143">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Almost a year later, in August 1981, <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> continued the study of the Saturn system. More close-up images of Saturn\'s moons were acquired, as well as evidence of changes in the atmosphere and the rings. Unfortunately, during the flyby, the probe\'s turnable camera platform stuck for a couple of days and some planned imaging was lost. Saturn\'s gravity was used to direct the spacecraft\'s trajectory towards Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voyager_143-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-143">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The probes discovered and confirmed several new satellites orbiting near or within the planet\'s rings, as well as the small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell_Gap" title="Maxwell Gap">Maxwell Gap</a> (a gap within the <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#C_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">C Ring</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keeler_gap" title="Keeler gap">Keeler gap</a> (a 42&#160;km wide gap in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A_Ring" title="A Ring">A Ring</a>).\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Cassini.E2.80.93Huygens_spacecraft"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cassini&#8211;Huygens_spacecraft"><i>Cassini&#8211;Huygens</i> spacecraft</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini&#8211;Huygens</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a> entered orbit around Saturn on 1 July 2004. In June 2004, it conducted a close flyby of <a href="/wiki/Phoebe_(moon)" title="Phoebe (moon)">Phoebe</a>, sending back high-resolution images and data. <i>Cassini</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> flyby of Saturn\'s largest moon, Titan, captured radar images of large lakes and their coastlines with numerous islands and mountains. The orbiter completed two Titan flybys before releasing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huygens_probe" title="Huygens probe"><i>Huygens</i> probe</a> on 25 December 2004. <i>Huygens</i> descended onto the surface of Titan on 14 January 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature438_7069_758_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature438_7069_758-144">[142]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Starting in early 2005, scientists used <i>Cassini</i> to track lightning on Saturn. The power of the lightning is approximately 1,000&#160;times that of lightning on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[143]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="977" data-file-width="1580" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg/220px-Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg/330px-Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg/440px-Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Enceladus_geysers_June_2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>At Enceladus\'s south pole geysers spray water from many locations along the <a href="/wiki/Tiger_stripes_(Enceladus)" title="Tiger stripes (Enceladus)">tiger stripes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728-146">[144]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In 2006, NASA reported that <i>Cassini</i> had found evidence of liquid water reservoirs no more than tens of meters below the surface that erupt in <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a> on Saturn\'s moon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>. These jets of icy particles are emitted into orbit around Saturn from vents in the moon\'s south polar region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[145]</a></sup> Over 100 geysers have been identified on Enceladus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20140728_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20140728-146">[144]</a></sup> In May 2011, NASA scientists reported that Enceladus "is emerging as the most habitable spot beyond Earth in the Solar System for life as we know it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Cassini</i> photographs have revealed a previously undiscovered planetary ring, outside the brighter main rings of Saturn and inside the G and E&#160;rings. The source of this ring is hypothesized to be the crashing of a meteoroid off <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">Janus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">Epimetheus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[148]</a></sup> In July 2006, images were returned of hydrocarbon lakes near Titan\'s north pole, the presence of which were confirmed in January 2007. In March 2007, hydrocarbon seas were found near the North pole, the largest of which is almost the size of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[149]</a></sup> In October 2006, the probe detected an 8,000&#160;km diameter cyclone-like storm with an eyewall at Saturn\'s south pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[150]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From 2004 to 2 November 2009, the probe discovered and confirmed eight new satellites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[151]</a></sup> In April 2013 <i>Cassini</i> sent back images of a hurricane at the planet\'s north pole 20 times larger than those found on Earth, with winds faster than 530&#160;km/h (330&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[152]</a></sup> On 15 September 2017, the <i>Cassini-Huygens</i> spacecraft performed the "Grand Finale" of its mission: a number of passes through gaps between Saturn and Saturn\'s inner rings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20170915_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20170915-155">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170914_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170914-156">[154]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric entry</a> of <i>Cassini</i> ended the mission.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_future_missions">Possible future missions</span></h4>\n<p>The continued exploration of Saturn is still considered to be a viable option for NASA as part of their ongoing New Frontiers program of missions. NASA previously requested for plans to be put forward for a mission to Saturn that included an atmospheric entry probe and possible investigations into the habitability and possible discovery of life on Saturn\'s moons Titan and Enceladus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[155]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="440" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg/220px-Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg/330px-Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c0/Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn-27-03-04.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Amateur telescopic view of Saturn</div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn is the most distant of the five planets easily visible to the naked eye from Earth, the other four being <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> and Jupiter. (Uranus and occasionally <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a> are visible to the naked eye in dark skies.) Saturn appears to the naked eye in the night sky as a bright, yellowish point of light. The mean <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a>  of Saturn is 0.46 with a standard deviation of 0.34.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-13">[11]</a></sup> Most of the magnitude variation is due to the inclination of the ring system relative to the Sun and Earth. The brightest magnitude, -0.55, occurs near in time to when the plane of the rings is inclined most highly, and the faintest magnitude, 1.17, occurs around the time when they are least inclined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-13">[11]</a></sup> It takes approximately 29.5 years for the planet to complete an entire circuit of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> against the background constellations of the <a href="/wiki/Zodiac" title="Zodiac">zodiac</a>. Most people will require an optical aid (very large binoculars or a small telescope) that magnifies at least 30 times to achieve an image of Saturn\'s rings, in which clear resolution is present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NMM_Saturn_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-NMM_Saturn-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-binoculars_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-binoculars-134">[132]</a></sup>  Twice every Saturnian year (roughly every 15 Earth years), the rings briefly disappear from view, due to the way in which they are angled and because they are so thin. Such a "disappearance" will next occur in 2025, but Saturn will be too close to the Sun for any ring-crossing observation to be possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[156]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturnoppositions-animated.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="112" data-file-width="175" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Saturnoppositions-animated.gif" width="175"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturnoppositions-animated.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Simulated appearance of Saturn as seen from Earth (at opposition) during an orbit of Saturn, 2001-2029</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1364" data-file-width="2766" decoding="async" height="108" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/220px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/330px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Saturn_eclipse.jpg/440px-Saturn_eclipse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saturn_eclipse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Saturn eclipses the Sun, as seen from <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i>. The rings are visible, including the F Ring.</div></div></div>\n<p>Saturn and its rings are best seen when the planet is at, or near, <a href="/wiki/Opposition_(planets)" title="Opposition (planets)">opposition</a>, the configuration of a planet when it is at an <a href="/wiki/Elongation_(astronomy)" title="Elongation (astronomy)">elongation</a> of 180&#176;, and thus appears opposite the Sun in the sky. A Saturnian opposition occurs every year&#8212;approximately every 378 days&#8212;and results in the planet appearing at its brightest. Both the Earth and Saturn orbit the Sun on eccentric orbits, which means their distances from the Sun vary over time, and therefore so do their distances from each other, hence varying the brightness of Saturn from one opposition to the next.  Saturn also appears brighter when the rings are angled such that they are more visible.  For example, during the opposition of 17 December 2002, Saturn appeared at its brightest due to a favorable <a href="/wiki/File:Saturnoppositions.jpg" title="File:Saturnoppositions.jpg">orientation of its rings</a> relative to the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opp2002_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-opp2002-159">[157]</a></sup> even though Saturn was closer to the Earth and Sun in late 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-opp2002_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-opp2002-159">[157]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From time to time Saturn is <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occulted</a> by the Moon (that is, the Moon covers up Saturn in the sky). As with all the planets in the Solar System, occultations of Saturn occur in "seasons". Saturnian occultations will take place 12 or more times over a 12-month period, followed by about a five-year period in which no such activity is registered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jon-160">[158]</a></sup> Australian astronomy experts Hill and Horner explain the seasonal nature of Saturnian occultations:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>This is the result of the fact that the moon&#8217;s orbit around the Earth is tilted to the orbit of the Earth around the Sun &#8211; and so most of the time, the moon will pass above or below Saturn in the sky, and no occultation will occur. It is only when Saturn lies near the point that the moon&#8217;s orbit crosses the "plane of the ecliptic" that occultations can happen &#8211; and then they occur every time the moon swings by, until Saturn moves away from the crossing point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jon_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jon-160">[158]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:802px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn,_Annotated_Version.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1250" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn%2C_Annotated_Version.jpg/800px-PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn%2C_Annotated_Version.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn%2C_Annotated_Version.jpg/1200px-PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn%2C_Annotated_Version.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn%2C_Annotated_Version.jpg/1600px-PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn%2C_Annotated_Version.jpg 2x" width="800"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA17218_%E2%80%93_A_Farewell_to_Saturn,_Annotated_Version.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><center>Farewell to Saturn and moons (<a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">Enceladus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epimetheus_(moon)" title="Epimetheus (moon)">Epimetheus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Janus_(moon)" title="Janus (moon)">Janus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">Mimas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pandora_(moon)" title="Pandora (moon)">Pandora</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prometheus_(moon)" title="Prometheus (moon)">Prometheus</a>), by <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i> (21 November 2017).</center></div></div></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_6-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-1bar_6-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a
